<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A high-quality genome sequence of Rosa chinensis to elucidate ornamental traits"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2018 4:7"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-06-11"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Rose is the world&#8217;s most important ornamental plant, with economic, cultural and symbolic value. Roses are cultivated worldwide and sold as garden roses, cut flowers and potted plants. Roses are outbred and can have various ploidy levels. Our objectives were to develop a high-quality reference genome sequence for the genus Rosa by sequencing a doubled haploid, combining long and short reads, and anchoring to a high-density genetic map, and to study the genome structure and genetic basis of major ornamental traits. We produced a doubled haploid rose line (&#8216;HapOB&#8217;) from Rosa chinensis &#8216;Old Blush&#8217; and generated a rose genome assembly anchored to seven pseudo-chromosomes (512&#8201;Mb with N50 of 3.4&#8201;Mb and 564 contigs). The length of 512&#8201;Mb represents 90.1&#8211;96.1% of the estimated haploid genome size of rose. Of the assembly, 95% is contained in only 196 contigs. The anchoring was validated using high-density diploid and tetraploid genetic maps. We delineated hallmark chromosomal features, including the pericentromeric regions, through annotation of transposable element families and positioned centromeric repeats using fluorescent in situ hybridization. The rose genome displays extensive synteny with the Fragaria vesca genome, and we delineated only two major rearrangements. Genetic diversity was analysed using resequencing data of seven diploid and one tetraploid Rosa species selected from various sections of the genus. Combining genetic and genomic approaches, we identified potential genetic regulators of key ornamental traits, including prickle density and the number of flower petals. A rose APETALA2/TOE homologue is proposed to be the major regulator of petal number in rose. This reference sequence is an important resource for studying polyploidization, meiosis and developmental processes, as we demonstrated for flower and prickle development. It will also accelerate breeding through the development of molecular markers linked to traits, the identification of the genes underlying them and the exploitation of synteny across Rosaceae. A high-quality reference genome of rose is generated by sequencing a doubled haploid line. The researchers analysed the genome structure and uncovered candidate genes associated with major ornamental traits through integrated genetic and genomic analyses."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Rose is the world’s most important ornamental plant, with economic, cultural and symbolic value. Roses are cultivated worldwide and sold as garden roses, cut flowers and potted plants. Roses are outbred and can have various ploidy levels. Our objectives were to develop a high-quality reference genome sequence for the genus <i>Rosa</i> by sequencing a doubled haploid, combining long and short reads, and anchoring to a high-density genetic map, and to study the genome structure and genetic basis of major ornamental traits. We produced a doubled haploid rose line (‘HapOB’) from <i>Rosa chinensis</i> ‘Old Blush’ and generated a rose genome assembly anchored to seven pseudo-chromosomes (512 Mb with N50 of 3.4 Mb and 564 contigs). The length of 512 Mb represents 90.1–96.1% of the estimated haploid genome size of rose. Of the assembly, 95% is contained in only 196 contigs. The anchoring was validated using high-density diploid and tetraploid genetic maps. We delineated hallmark chromosomal features, including the pericentromeric regions, through annotation of transposable element families and positioned centromeric repeats using fluorescent in situ hybridization. The rose genome displays extensive synteny with the <i>Fragaria vesca</i> genome, and we delineated only two major rearrangements. Genetic diversity was analysed using resequencing data of seven diploid and one tetraploid <i>Rosa</i> species selected from various sections of the genus. Combining genetic and genomic approaches, we identified potential genetic regulators of key ornamental traits, including prickle density and the number of flower petals. A rose <i>APETALA2/TOE</i> homologue is proposed to be the major regulator of petal number in rose. This reference sequence is an important resource for studying polyploidization, meiosis and developmental processes, as we demonstrated for flower and prickle development. It will also accelerate breeding through the development of molecular markers linked to traits, the identification of the genes underlying them and the exploitation of synteny across Rosaceae.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Rose is the queen of flowers, holding great symbolic and cultural value. Roses appeared as decoration on 5,000-year-old Asian pottery<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wang, G. A study on the history of Chinese roses from ancient works and images. Acta Hortic. 751, 347–356 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39109e942">1</a></sup>, and Romans cultivated roses for their flowers and essential oil<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Pliny (2013) Pine L'Ancience: Histoire naturelle (Schmit, S., Trans) Bibliothèque de la Pléiade No. 593 (Gallimard, Paris, 2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39109e946">2</a></sup>. Today, no ornamental plants have greater economic importance than roses. They are cultivated worldwide and are sold as garden plants, in pots or as cut flowers, the latter accounting for approximately 30% of the market. Roses are also used for scent production and for culinary purposes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Nybom, H. &amp; Werlemark, G. Realizing the potential of health-promoting rosehips from dogroses (Rosa sect. Caninae). Curr. Bioact. Compd. 13, 3–17 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39109e950">3</a></sup>.</p><p>Despite their genetic complexity and lack of biotechnological resources, rose represents a model for ornamental plant species, allowing the investigation of traits such as bloom seasonality or flower morphology. Furthermore, rose displays a range of unique features as a result of its complex evolutionary and breeding history, including interspecific hybridization events and polyploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, J. et al. The diploid origins of allopolyploid rose species studied using single nucleotide polymorphism haplotypes flanking a microsatellite repeat. J. Hortic. Sci. Biotechnol. 88, 85–92 (2013)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39109e957">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ritz, C. M. &amp; Wisseman, V. Microsatellite analyses of artificial and spontaneous dogroses hybrids reveal the hybridogenic origin of Rosa micrantha by the contribution of unreduced gametes. J. Hered. 102, 2117–2127 (2011)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39109e957_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Meng, J., Fougère-Danezan, M., Zhang, L.-B., Li, D.-Z. &amp; Yi, T.-S. Untangling the hybrid origin of the Chinese tea roses: evidence from DNA sequences of single-copy nuclear and chloroplast genes. Plant Syst. Evol. 297, 157–170 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39109e960">6</a></sup>. Roses belong to the genus <i>Rosa</i> (Rosoideae, Rosaceae), which contains more than 150 species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wisseman, V. &amp; Ritz, C. M. The genus Rosa (Rosoideae, Rosaceae) revisited: molecular analysis of nrITS-1 and atpB-rbcL intergenic spacer (IGS) versus conventional taxonomy. Bot. J. Linna. Soc. 147, 275–290 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39109e967">7</a></sup> of varying ploidy levels, ranging from 2n = 2× to 10×<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Jian, H. et al. Decaploidy in Rosa praelucens Byhouwer (Rosaceae) endemic to Zhongdian Plateau, Yunnan, China. Caryologia. 63, 162–167 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39109e971">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Robert, A. V., Gladis, T. &amp; Brumme, H. DNA amounts of roses (Rosa L.) and their use in attributing ploidy levels. Plant Cell Rep. 28, 61–71 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39109e974">9</a></sup>. Many modern roses are tetraploid and can be genetically classified as ‘segmental’ allopolyploids (a mixture between allopolyploidy and autopolyploidy)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bourke, P. M. et al. Partial preferential chromosome pairing is genotype dependent in tetraploid rose. Plant J. 90, 330–343 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39109e978">10</a></sup>, whereas dog-roses display unequal meiosis to maintain pentaploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Herklotz, V. &amp; Ritz, C. M. Multiple and asymmetrical origin of polyploid dog rose hybrids (Rosa L. sect. Caninae (DC.) Ser.) involving unreduced gametes. Ann. Bot. 120, 209–220 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39109e983">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Ritz, C. M., Köhnen, I., Groth, M., Theissen, G. &amp; Wisseman, V. To be or not to be the odd one out—allele-specific transcription in pentaploid dogroses (Rosa L. sect. Caninae (DC.) Ser). BMC Plant Biol. 11, 37 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39109e986">12</a></sup>. Rose breeding has a long and generally unresolved history in Europe and Asia, most likely involving several interspecific hybridization events. Importantly, many very-old varieties are still maintained in private and public rose gardens and are a living historical archive of rose breeding and selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Liorzou, M. et al. Nineteenth century French rose (Rosa sp.) germplasm shows a shift over time from a European to an Asian genetic background. J. Exp. Bot. 67, 4711–4725 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39109e990">13</a></sup>. Large and well-documented herbarium collections, combined with genomic advances, offer excellent opportunities to reconstruct phylogenetic relationships within the species.</p><p>Roses have been subject to selection for several traits that are not usually encountered in other crops. In particular, aesthetic criteria have been a principal focus of rose breeding over the past 250 years, next to plant vigour and resistances to biotic and abiotic stresses. Among the aesthetic traits, flower colour and architecture (from 5-petalled ‘simple’ flowers to 100-petalled ‘double’ flowers), floral scent and prickle formation on the stem and leaves have been the main targets of the breeders’ eyes (and noses). Although these traits can be interpreted as signs of the domestication process, they originally evolved through adaptation to natural conditions. The availability of a high-quality reference genome sequence is key to unravelling the genetic basis underlying these evolutionary and developmental processes that accelerate future genetic, genomic, transcriptomic and epigenetic analyses. Recently, a draft reference genome sequence of <i>Rosa multiflora</i> has been published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nakamura, N. et al. Genome structure of Rosa multiflora, a wild ancestor of cultivated roses. DNA Res. 25, 113–121 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39109e1000">14</a></sup>. Although completeness measures suggest that the assembly is fairly complete in terms of the gene space covered, it is also highly fragmented (83,189 scaffolds, N50 of 90 kb).</p><p>Here, we present an annotated high-quality reference genome sequence for the <i>Rosa</i> genus using a haploid rose line derived from an old Chinese <i>Rosa chinensis</i> variety ‘Old Blush’ (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig1">1a</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig1">b</a>). ‘Old Blush’ (syn. Parsons’ Pink China) was brought to Europe and North America in the eighteenth century from China and is one of the most influential genotypes in the history of rose breeding. Among other things, it introduced recurrent flowering into Western germplasm, which is an essential trait for the development of modern rose cultivars<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wylie, A. P. The history of garden roses. J. R. Hortic. Soc. 79, 555–571 (1954)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39109e1019">15</a></sup>. We validated our pseudo-chromosome scale genome assembly of ‘Old Blush’ using high-density genetic maps of multiple F1 progenies and synteny with <i>Fragaria vesca</i>. We delineated hallmark chromosomal features, such as the pericentromeric regions, through annotation of transposable element families and positioning of centromeric repeats using fluorescent in situ hybridization (FISH). This reference genome also allowed us to analyse the genetic diversity within the <i>Rosa</i> genus following a resequencing of eight wild species. Using genetic (F1 progeny and diversity panel) and genomic approaches, we were able to identify key potential genetic regulators of important ornamental traits, including continuous flowering, flower development, prickle density and self-incompatibility.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Development of the HapOB haploid line from R. chinensis ‘Old Blush’."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Development of the HapOB haploid line from <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> ‘Old Blush’.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0166-1/MediaObjects/41477_2018_166_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0166-1/MediaObjects/41477_2018_166_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="152" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> variety ‘Old Blush’ painted by Redouté in 1817. Paul Fearn/Alamy Stock Photo. <b>b</b>, A flower from the <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> variety ‘Old Blush’. <b>c</b>, A cross-section of the floral stage used for the anther culture. <b>d</b>, DAPI staining on mid-to-late uninucleate microspores. Similar results were observed on more than 15 microspores in one experiment. <b>e</b>, The HapOB callus was obtained after the anther culture at the appropriate stage and used for genome sequencing.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Development of a high-quality reference genome sequence</h3><p>We developed a haploid callus cell line (HapOB) using an anther culture at the mid-to-late uninucleate microspore developmental stage from the diploid heterozygous ‘Old Blush’ variety (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig1">1c</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig1">e</a>). The homozygosity of the HapOB line was verified with ten microsatellite markers distributed over the seven linkage groups (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM3">1</a>). Flow cytometric analysis showed the HapOB callus to be diploid, suggesting that spontaneous genome doubling occurred during in vitro propagation.</p><p>A combination of Illumina short-read sequencing and PacBio long-read sequencing technologies was used to assemble the doubled haploid HapOB genome sequence. PacBio sequencing data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM4">2</a>) was assembled with CANU<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39109e1111">16</a></sup>, yielding 551 contigs (N50 of 3.4 Mb), representing a total length of 512 Mb. Of the obtained sequence, 95% is contained in only 196 contigs. The PacBio-based assembly was error corrected with Illumina paired-end reads: 37,300 single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) and 307,700 insertions and deletions (indels) were corrected, representing 341.1 kb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM4">2</a>). K-mer spectrum analysis (K = 25) suggested a genome size of 532.7 Mb (251.1 Mb of a unique genome sequence and 279.6 Mb of repetitive sequences), whereas flow cytometric analysis estimated a genome size of 1C = 568 ± 9 Mb. Thus, the assembled sequence represents 96.1% or 90.1%, respectively, of the estimated genome size. No major contamination was detected by screening for the predicted prokaryotic genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM5">3</a>). Furthermore, only four contigs had low Illumina read mapping frequency, all of which were found to most likely encode plant proteins.</p><p>High-density female and male genetic maps were developed from a cross between <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> ‘Old Blush’ and a hybrid of <i>Rosa wichurana</i> (OW). F1 progeny from this cross (<i>n</i> = 151) were genotyped with the WagRhSNP 68K Axiom array<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Koning-Boucoiran, C. F. et al. Using RNA-seq to assemble a rose transcriptome with more than 13,000 full-length expressed genes and to develop the WagRhSNP 68k Axiom SNP array for rose (Rosa L.). Front. Plant Sci. 6, 249 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d39109e1136">17</a></sup> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM6">4</a>). Thirteen contigs, for which marker order clearly indicated assembly artefacts, were split before anchoring all 564 resulting contigs to the female and male genetic maps using a total of 6,746 SNP markers (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Tab1">1</a>). Of these, 196 contigs were anchored manually onto the seven linkage groups, mostly on both the female and the male genetic maps (174 and 143 contigs, respectively). In total, 466 Mb were therefore anchored onto the genetic maps and assembled into 7 pseudo-chromosomes representing 90% of the assembled contig length (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">1a</a>). The remaining 368 contigs (52 Mb) were assigned to chromosome 0 (Chr0). The quality of the assembly of the seven pseudo-chromosomes was assessed using two independent genetic maps: the previously published integrated high-density genetic map (K5) based on 172 tetraploid F1 progeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bourke, P. M. et al. Partial preferential chromosome pairing is genotype dependent in tetraploid rose. Plant J. 90, 330–343 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39109e1156">10</a></sup>, and a newly developed high-density map based on 174 diploid F1 progeny from a cross between cultivar ‘Yesterday’ and <i>R.</i> <i>wichurana</i> (YW; see Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">1b</a>). The co-linearity between the pseudo-chromosomes and both linkage maps is excellent (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">2</a>). In addition, the anchoring of the 386 contigs (52 Mb), currently assigned to Chr0, onto the K5 map and the YW map revealed that 39 contigs (total: 28.4 Mb) and 27 contigs (total: 24.1 Mb), respectively, can potentially be positioned onto the 7 linkage groups (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">2</a>). However, because these genetic maps were created using independent genotypes that are not related to <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> ‘Old Blush’, we chose a conservative approach by not incorporating these contigs into the pseudo-chromosome sequence of HapOB.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Metrics of the alignment of the male and female genetic maps with the HapOB genome assembly</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Positioning centromeres within the genome assembly</h3><p>The centromeric regions were identified using both bioinformatic and cytogenetic methods. We discovered a highly abundant tandem repeat (0.06% of the genome with more than 2,000 copies per haploid genome) of monomers (159 bp long) that we call OBC226 (‘Old Blush’ centromeric repeat from RepeatExplorer cluster 226; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig2">2a</a>). PCR confirmed the tandem organization of this repeat (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig2">2b</a>). FISH analysis unambiguously confirmed the location of the repeat in the centromeric regions of four of the seven chromosomes: Chr2, Chr5, Chr6 and Chr7 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig2">2c</a>). Mapping of the OBC226 repeat sequence revealed regions with high coverage on all HapOB pseudo-chromosomes except Chr1, which explains why no clear centromeric region could be detected on this chromosome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig2">2d</a>). On Chr3 and Chr4, the copy number of OBC226 was probably too low to be detected by FISH. Furthermore, the core OBC226 centromeric repeats were flanked by other repetitive sequences, and these were unequally distributed along the chromosomes, with a clearly higher density in the core centromeric regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig2">2d</a>). These centromeric regions were also enriched in Ty3/<i>Gypsy</i> transposable elements. Taken together, these results confirm the position of the centromeric regions on the seven pseudo-chromosomes and reveal the high repeat sequence content, and low gene content, of the scaffolds currently assigned to Chr0.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Identification of centromeric regions in the HapOB reference genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Identification of centromeric regions in the HapOB reference genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0166-1/MediaObjects/41477_2018_166_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0166-1/MediaObjects/41477_2018_166_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="407" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The cluster CL226 identified by RepeatExplorer. <b>b</b>, Agarose gel electrophoresis of tandem repeat fragments amplified from the genomic DNA of HapOB using OBC226 PCR primers (right lane) along with the lambda-<i>Pst</i>I size ladder (left lane). Similar results were obtained in two independent experiments. <b>c</b>, FISH with carboxy tetramethylrhodamine (TAMRA)-labelled OBC226 oligo probes on <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> metaphase chromosomes. Chromosome numbers are labelled from 1 to 7. Similar results were observed in at least 10 metaphase cells in two independent experiments. <b>d</b>, Circos representation of the distribution of OBC226 (purple), the pericentromeric region (blue), Ty3/<i>Gypsy</i> (orange) and Ty1/<i>Copia</i> repeat elements (green) along the seven pseudo-chromosomes and Chr0 (scale in Mb).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Annotation of the sequence</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec6">Coding genes</h4><p>Based on the mapping of 723,268 transcript sequences (expressed sequence tag/complementary DNA and RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) contigs with a minimum size of 150 bp) onto the HapOB genome assembly, we predicted a total of 44,481 genes covering 21% of the genomic sequence length using Eugene combiner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Foissac, S. et al. Genome annotation in plants and fungi: EuGene as a model platform. Curr. Bioinform. 3, 87–97 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2056">18</a></sup>. These include 39,669 protein-coding genes and 4,812 non-coding genes. Evidence of transcription was found for 87.8% of all predicted genes. At least one InterPro domain signature was detected in 86.5% of the protein-coding genes using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2060">19</a></sup>, with 68.0% of the genes assigned to 4,051 PFAM gene families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Finn, R. D. et al. The Pfam protein families database: towards a more sustainable future. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D279–D285 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2064">20</a></sup>. The quality of the structural annotation was assessed using the BUSCO v2 method based on a benchmark of 1,440 conserved plant genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2068">21</a></sup>, of which 92.5% had complete gene coverage (including 5.3% duplicated ones), 4.1% were fragmented and only 3.4% were missing. This result can be compared to the analysis of the whole-genome assembly, which identified 95% complete genes and 3.6% missing genes. The set of predicted non-coding genes included 186 ribosomal RNA, 751 transfer RNA, 384 small nucleolar RNA, 99 microRNA, 170 small nuclear RNA and 3,222 unclassified genes (annotated as non-coding RNA) with evidence of transcription but no consistent coding sequence.</p><p>The number of predicted proteins in <i>Rosa</i> (39,669) is higher than the number of predicted proteins in <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> (28,588 predicted proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. GigaScience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2084">22</a></sup>). By BLAST analysis, we identified 6,543 proteins that are rose specific. Among them, 5,867 proteins have no homologue in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. For these proteins, no functional information is available from closely related species and experimental evidence will be required to explore their role in roses. We also looked at whether the difference in the predicted number of proteins was owing to protein family expansion. Such a scenario was detected for some protein families, including nucleotide-binding site leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR) and cytochrome P450 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec7">Transposable elements</h4><p>The REPET package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Flutre, T., Duprat, E., Feuillet, C. &amp; Quesneville, H. Considering transposable element diversification in de novo annotation approaches. PLoS ONE 6, e16526 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2103">23</a></sup> was used to generate a genome-wide annotation of repetitive sequences of the HapOB genome (see Methods for details). Retrotransposons, also called class I elements, represent the largest transposable element genomic fraction (35.1% of the sequenced genome), of which long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons represent 28.3%. <i>Gypsy</i> elements are more frequent than <i>Copia</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM7">5a</a>). Non-LTR retrotransposons long intersperced nuclear elements (LINEs) and potential short intersperced nuclear elements (SINEs) represent 5.0% of the sequence genome and class II elements (DNA transposons and Helitrons) represent 11.7% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM7">5a</a>). The remaining 15% include unclassified repeats (7.3%), chimaeric consensus sequences (1.9%) and potential repeated host genes (5.8%). We also identified Caulimoviridae copies representing 1.25% of the genome. Interestingly, one particularly abundant <i>Gypsy</i> Tat-like family was found in the genome assembly. The total copy coverage represents 3.4% of the genome. Tat-like elements are known to have an open reading frame (ORF) after the polymerase domains, and surprisingly in this case, the ORF corresponds to a class II transposase domain.</p><p>In a preliminary comparison between the transposable element annotation in HapOB and the <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> v2.0.a1 genome assembly (without manual curation) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM7">5b</a>), we found that retrotransposon elements represent the largest transposable element genomic fraction in <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> (13.91%), similar to rose. We found approximately twofold more copies for all transposable element families except SINE and unclassified in <i>Rosa</i> than in <i>Fragaria</i>. This indicates that the difference in genome size between <i>Rosa</i> and <i>Fragaria</i> is largely due to an expansion of the transposable element fraction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Synteny between <i>Rosa</i> and <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i>
                        </h3><p><i>Rosa</i> and <i>Fragaria</i> both belong to the Rosoideae subfamily of the Rosaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Potter, D. et al. Phylogeny and classification of Rosaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 266, 5–43 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2178">24</a></sup>, having diverged around 50 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Xiang, Y. et al. Evolution of Rosaceae fruit types based on nuclear phylogeny in the context of geological times and genome duplication. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 262–281 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2182">25</a></sup>. Previous genetic studies have demonstrated that large macrosyntenic blocks are conserved between <i>Rosa</i> and <i>Fragaria</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bourke, P. M. et al. Partial preferential chromosome pairing is genotype dependent in tetraploid rose. Plant J. 90, 330–343 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2192">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Gar, O. et al. An autotetraploid linkage map of rose (Rosa hybrida) validated using the strawberry (Fragaria vesca) genome sequence. PLoS ONE 6, e20463 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2195">26</a></sup>. We compared the HapOB genome to the recently updated <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. GigaScience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2205">22</a></sup> to analyse the synteny in detail (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">4a</a>). <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> Chrs 1, 4, 5, 6 and 7 display strong synteny with <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> Chrs 7, 4, 3, 2 and 5, respectively. Consistent with previous suggestions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bourke, P. M. et al. Partial preferential chromosome pairing is genotype dependent in tetraploid rose. Plant J. 90, 330–343 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2225">10</a></sup>, a reciprocal translocation was detected between <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> Chr 2 and 3 and <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> Chrs 6 and 1, respectively. Our results clarify the highly conserved synteny between <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> and <i>Rosa</i>, revealing only two major translocation events.</p><p>Within the Rosaceae family, the synteny is also well conserved between <i>Prunus</i> and <i>Rosa</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">4c</a>): <i>Rosa</i> Chr1 corresponds to <i>Prunus</i> Chr2, <i>Rosa</i> Chr4 corresponds to the end of <i>Prunus</i> Chr1, whereas <i>Prunus</i> Chrs 3, 5 and 8 correspond to large parts of <i>Rosa</i> Chrs 2, 6 and 7 respectively. Owing to the allopolyploid origin of <i>Malus</i>, the overall synteny is less clear, even if large blocks of synteny can be detected (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">4b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genetic diversity with the genus <i>Rosa</i>
                        </h3><p>The 150 or more existing rose species belong to four subgenera. Excluding the subgenus <i>Rosa</i>, all subgenera contain only one or two species. We resequenced eight <i>Rosa</i> species (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Tab2">2</a>), representing three of the four subgenera (<i>Hulthemia</i>: <i>R.</i> <i>persica</i>, <i>Herperhodos</i>: <i>R.</i> <i>minutifolia</i> and <i>Rosa</i>). Within <i>Rosa</i>, we covered all of the main sections according to the latest phylogenetic analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bruneau, A., Starr, J. R. &amp; Joly, S. Phylogenetic relationships in the genus Rosa: new evidence from chloroplast DNA sequences and an appraisal of current knowledge. Syst. Bot. 32, 366–378 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2336">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Fougère-Danezan, M., Joly, S., Bruneau, A., Gao, X.-F. &amp; Zhang, L.-B. Phylogeny and biogeography of wild roses with specific attention to polyploids. Ann. Bot. 115, 275–291 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39109e2339">28</a></sup> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Tab2">2</a>) in the form of <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> var. <i>spontanea</i>, <i>R.</i> <i>rugosa</i>, <i>R.</i> <i>laevigata, R.</i> <i>moschata, R.</i> <i>xanthina spontanea</i> and <i>R.</i> <i>gallica</i>. All are diploid species except <i>R.</i> <i>gallica</i>, which is tetraploid (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Tab2">2</a>). SNPs and indels were identified relative to the HapOB reference sequence (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Summary of resequencing and sequence variations (SNP and small indels) identified in eight <i>Rosa</i> species</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Resequencing of eight Rosa species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Resequencing of eight <i>Rosa</i> species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0166-1/MediaObjects/41477_2018_166_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0166-1/MediaObjects/41477_2018_166_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="962" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The phylogenetic relationships of the eight sequenced <i>Rosa</i> species and the reference genome HapOB, using a genome-wide set of homozygous SNPs. <b>b</b>, Analysis of genetic diversity in eight species of the <i>Rosa</i> genus along the seven pseudo-chromosomes of the HapOB reference sequence. Circles from outside to inside show: gene density (red), transposable element density (green), SNP density for <i>R.</i> <i>xantina</i> (purple), <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> var. <i>spontanea</i> (yellow), <i>R.</i> <i>gallica</i> (blue), <i>R.</i> <i>laevigata</i> (light green), <i>R.</i> <i>moschata</i> (light orange), <i>R.</i> <i>rugosa</i> (light purple), <i>R.</i> <i>persica</i> (light red) and <i>R.</i> <i>minutifolia alba</i> (light blue). Scales are in Mb.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The nuclear SNP-based phylogenetic tree of the eight species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig3">3a</a>) is consistent with previous molecular analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bruneau, A., Starr, J. R. &amp; Joly, S. Phylogenetic relationships in the genus Rosa: new evidence from chloroplast DNA sequences and an appraisal of current knowledge. Syst. Bot. 32, 366–378 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3504">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Fougère-Danezan, M., Joly, S., Bruneau, A., Gao, X.-F. &amp; Zhang, L.-B. Phylogeny and biogeography of wild roses with specific attention to polyploids. Ann. Bot. 115, 275–291 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3507">28</a></sup>. The clade, including <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i>, <i>R.</i> <i>gallica</i>, <i>R.</i> <i>moschata</i> and <i>R.</i> <i>laevigata</i>, fits with the <i>Synstylae</i> and allies clade previously found in a chloroplastic analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Fougère-Danezan, M., Joly, S., Bruneau, A., Gao, X.-F. &amp; Zhang, L.-B. Phylogeny and biogeography of wild roses with specific attention to polyploids. Ann. Bot. 115, 275–291 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3540">28</a></sup>. The same is the case for <i>R.</i> <i>persica</i> and <i>R.</i> <i>xanthina</i>, both belonging to the <i>Cinnamomeae</i> and allies clade. However, <i>R.</i> <i>rugosa</i> and <i>R.</i> <i>minutifolia</i> show an uncertain position. In particular, <i>R.</i> <i>minutifolia</i>, which belongs to the <i>Hesperhodos</i> subgenus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bruneau, A., Starr, J. R. &amp; Joly, S. Phylogenetic relationships in the genus Rosa: new evidence from chloroplast DNA sequences and an appraisal of current knowledge. Syst. Bot. 32, 366–378 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3582">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Fougère-Danezan, M., Joly, S., Bruneau, A., Gao, X.-F. &amp; Zhang, L.-B. Phylogeny and biogeography of wild roses with specific attention to polyploids. Ann. Bot. 115, 275–291 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3585">28</a></sup>, was expected to be closer to <i>R.</i> <i>persica</i> and <i>R.</i> <i>xanthina</i>. One of the possible explanations is that the resequenced <i>R.</i> <i>minutifolia</i> individual is actually the product of an interspecific cross, as it shows unexpected morphological characters, such as few prickles on the young flowering shoots and flowers clustering in inflorescences. Methodologically, the use of only homozygous SNPs may have caused bias, especially in <i>R.</i> <i>rugosa</i>, as most of its SNPs were in the heterozygous state (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM8">6</a>).</p><p>The lowest SNP and indel density was found in <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> var. <i>spontanea</i> (9.9 and 1.6 per kb, respectively). ‘Old Blush’ is described as an interspecific cross between <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> var. <i>spontanea</i> and <i>R</i>. x <i>odorata</i> var. <i>gigantea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Meng, J., Fougère-Danezan, M., Zhang, L.-B., Li, D.-Z. &amp; Yi, T.-S. Untangling the hybrid origin of the Chinese tea roses: evidence from DNA sequences of single-copy nuclear and chloroplast genes. Plant Syst. Evol. 297, 157–170 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3647">6</a></sup>, which is consistent with the relatively low sequence divergence of <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i> var. <i>spontanea</i> compared to the HapOB reference sequence. The highest SNP and indel density was found in <i>R.</i> <i>gallica</i> (21.0 and 4.5 per kb, respectively); this could be the result of the (allo)tetraploidy of this species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Fernández-Romero, M. D., Torres, A. M., Millán, T., Cubero, J. I. &amp; Cabrera, A. Physical mapping of ribosomal DNA on several species of the subgenus Rosa. Theor. Appl. Genet. 103, 835–838 (2001)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3667">29</a></sup>, as shown by its high proportion of heterozygous SNPs (74%; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM8">6</a>).</p><p>As expected, the majority (79.2–89.0%) of the SNPs were located in non-coding regions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM8">6</a>). Only 3–7% of the SNPs were located in exons, of which half were synonymous, in line with other species (for example, tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="The 100 Tomato Genome Sequencing Consortium et al. Exploring genetic variation in the tomato (Solanum section Lycopersicon) clade by whole-genome sequencing. Plant J. 80, 136–148 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3680">30</a></sup>). The different species displayed varying levels of homozygosity (homozygous SNPs ranging from 79.2% in <i>R.</i> <i>persica</i> to 26.0% in tetraploid <i>R.</i> <i>gallica</i>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM8">6</a>). The number of small indels was higher (between 876,648 and 2,430,123) than <i>Malus</i>, with an average of 346,498 indels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Duan, N. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the history of apple and supports a two-stage model for fruit enlargement. Nat. Commun. 8, 249 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3703">31</a></sup>, suggesting a higher level of diversity within the <i>Rosa</i> genus.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Analysis of the genetic control of important traits</h3><p>This new reference sequence is an important tool to help decipher the genetic basis of ornamental traits, such as blooming (including continuous flowering, flower development and the number of petals), prickle density on the stem and self-incompatibility. We studied the genetic determinism in (1) two F1 progenies (151 individuals obtained from the OW progeny and 174 individuals obtained from the YW progeny), and (2) a panel of 96 rose cultivars originating from the nineteenth to the twenty-first century<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Nguyen, T. H. N., Schulz, D., Winkelmann, T. &amp; Debener, T. Genetic dissection of adventitious shoot regeneration in roses by employing genome-wide association studies. Plant Cell Rep. 36, 1493–1505 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3718">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Schulz, D. F. et al. Genome-wide association analysis of the anthocyanin and carotenoid contents of rose petals. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 1798 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3721">33</a></sup>. Our data demonstrate that important loci controlling continuous flowering, double flower morphology, self-incompatibility and prickle density were predominantly localized in a single genomic region of Chr3 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig4">4a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="A region at the end of Chr3 controls important ornamental traits."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: A region at the end of Chr3 controls important ornamental traits.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0166-1/MediaObjects/41477_2018_166_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0166-1/MediaObjects/41477_2018_166_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="863" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Major genes and QTLs that control continuous flowering, double flower, self-incompatibility and prickle density are shown together with candidate genes for each trait. Detailed analyses per locus are described in Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">10</a>, respectively. For prickle density in OW progeny (OW2017 and OW2016), the boxes represent the 1-LOD (log of the odds ratio) interval and the lines the 2-LOD interval. <b>b</b>,<b>c</b>, GWAS analysis showing the <i>P</i> values of the association between SNPs positioned along Chr3 and the number of petals, indicating regions that control the number of petals. The petal number is considered as a qualitative trait (simple versus double flowers; GLM) (<b>b</b>) or as a quantitative trait (MLM) (<b>c</b>). The horizontal red line shows Bonferroni-corrected significance levels (1.78 × 10<sup>–6</sup>). Other significant associations detected by GWAS are shown in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">12</a>. <i>n</i> = 96 cultivars with 3 flowers scored by cultivar. <b>d,e</b>, QTL analysis for prickle density in two F1 progenies using the OW mapping population based on scoring from 2016 and 2017, n = 151 individuals (<b>d</b>), and the YW mapping population, <i>n</i> = 174 individuals (<b>e</b>). Lod, log likelihood ratio.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec11">Detection of a new allele controlling continuous flowering in rose</h4><p>Most species of roses are 'once flowering'. In rose, continuous flowering is controlled by a homologue of the <i>TERMINAL FLOWER 1</i> (<i>TFL1</i>) family, <i>RoKSN</i>, located on Chr3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Iwata, H. et al. The TFL1 homologue KSN is a regulator of continuous flowering in rose and strawberry. Plant J. 69, 116–125 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3815">34</a></sup>). The continuous flowering phenotype is due to the insertion of a <i>Copia</i> retrotransposon element in the <i>RoKSN</i> gene. The continuous flowering rose ‘Old Blush’ was previously proposed to be <i>RoKSN</i><sup><i>Copia</i></sup><i>/RoKSN</i><sup><i>Copia</i></sup> at the <i>RoKSN</i> locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Iwata, H. et al. The TFL1 homologue KSN is a regulator of continuous flowering in rose and strawberry. Plant J. 69, 116–125 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3840">34</a></sup>. This <i>Copia</i> element corresponds to the RLC_denovoHm-B-G10244-Map11 retrotransposon. We identified 34 insertions of this transposable element in the HapOB genome, of which 11 are full length (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM9">7a</a>). The element is inserted into three genes, all of which are disrupted. The 3′ and 5′ LTRs of the full-length elements are &gt;99% similar (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM9">7a</a>), suggesting a recent insertion, as previously proposed for the element inserted in <i>RoKSN</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Iwata, H. et al. The TFL1 homologue KSN is a regulator of continuous flowering in rose and strawberry. Plant J. 69, 116–125 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3856">34</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, quantitative trait locus (QTL) analysis in the OW progeny identified the <i>CONTINUOUS FLOWERING</i> locus on Chr3 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig4">4a</a>), as expected, but we were unable to detect the <i>RoKSN</i> gene in the annotated HapOB genome. Detailed analysis of <i>RoKSN</i> allele segregation in the OW progeny revealed the existence of a null allele, in which <i>RoKSN</i> is deleted (see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM10">8</a> for further details). The diploid ‘Old Blush’ parent of the OB mapping population is therefore hemizygous <i>RoKSN</i><sup><i>Copia</i></sup><i>/RoKSN</i><sup><i>null</i></sup>, and the <i>RoKSN</i><sup><i>null</i></sup> allele is present in the HapOB genome sequence.</p><p>Interesting parallels exist between rose and <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> because <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> also exhibits both the once flowering and the continuous flowering phenotypes. In strawberry, a 2-bp deletion in the <i>TFL1</i> homologue causes a shift from once flowering to continuous flowering<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Iwata, H. et al. The TFL1 homologue KSN is a regulator of continuous flowering in rose and strawberry. Plant J. 69, 116–125 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3918">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Koskela, E. A. et al. Mutation in TERMINAL FLOWER1 reverses the photoperiodic requirement for flowering in the wild strawberry Fragaria vesca. Plant Physiol. 159, 1043–1054 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3921">35</a></sup>. Synteny analysis revealed four orthologous syntenic blocks in the <i>RoKSN</i> gene region, here called blocks A–D (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">5</a>). We detected a pattern of conserved gene content in combination with genome rearrangements between different <i>Rosa</i> species and the published genome sequence of <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109–116 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3940">36</a></sup> where the synteny with <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> is broken at the <i>FvKSN</i> location. The <i>FvKSN</i> gene is located between the A and B blocks in <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i>. The A block is inverted in the HapOB genome, and the C and D blocks are inserted between the A and B blocks. In <i>R.</i> <i>multiflora</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nakamura, N. et al. Genome structure of Rosa multiflora, a wild ancestor of cultivated roses. DNA Res. 25, 113–121 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39109e3968">14</a></sup> and <i>R.</i> <i>laevigata</i> (see Methods for the partial <i>R.</i> <i>laevigata</i> genome sequence assembly), which are both once flowering, the <i>RoKSN</i><sup><i>WT</i></sup> allele is present and synteny is conserved with <i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">5</a>). Taken together, these data indicate that the <i>RoKSN</i><sup><i>null</i></sup> allele is the result of a large rearrangement at the <i>CONTINUOUS FLOWERING</i> locus, leading to the complete deletion of the <i>RoKSN</i> gene. The <i>RoKSN</i><sup><i>null</i></sup> allele represents a novel allele responsible for continuous flowering, which has not been previously described.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec12">Double flower</h4><p>The number of petals is an important ornamental trait, and roses with higher numbers of petal (‘double flower’) have traditionally been selected. Through a study of mutant lines (sports), the change in petal number was attributed to a homeotic conversion in organ identity, with stamens converted into petals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Dubois, A. et al. Tinkering with the C-function: a molecular frame for the selection of double flowers in cultivated roses. PLoS ONE 5, e9288 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4027">37</a></sup>. The genetic basis of the double flower trait is complex, with a dominant gene (<i>DOUBLE FLOWER</i>) controlling simple versus double flower phenotypes and two QTLs controlling the number of petals on double flowers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Roman, H. et al. Genetic analysis of the flowering date and number of petals in rose. Tree Genet. Genomes 11, 85 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4034">38</a></sup>. Here, we combined the genome sequence with segregation data of four different F1 progenies to confine the putative location of the <i>DOUBLE FLOWER</i> locus (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM11">9</a>) to a 293-kb region of Chr3 (between position 33.24 Mb and 33.53 Mb; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig4">4a</a>). Using a genome-wide association study (GWAS) approach with a panel of 96 cultivated roses, we detected a strong association with simple versus double flowers in the same region (between position 33.08 Mb and 33.94 Mb; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig4">4b</a>). A second significant peak was located at a distance of 5 Mb, which may correspond to a secondary locus influencing this trait.</p><p>The 293-kb region contains 41 annotated genes. Among these, half are expressed during the early stages of floral development (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM12">10</a>). By excluding genes expressed in later floral stages (with completely open flowers), we retained four candidate genes: an F-box protein (RC3G0245100), a homologue of <i>APETALA2/TOE</i> (RC3G0243000), a Ypt/Rab-GAP domain of the gyp1p superfamily protein (RC3G0245000) and a tetratricopeptide repeat-like superfamily protein (RC3G0243500) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM12">10</a>).</p><p>Concerning double flowering, ‘Old Blush’ is heterozygous for the <i>DOUBLE FLOWER</i> locus. Sequencing both alleles of the four selected candidate genes in ‘Old Blush’ revealed only minor modifications for RC3G0245100, RC3G0245000 and RC3G0243500 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">6a–c</a>, respectively). However, concerning the <i>APETALA2/TOE</i> gene (RC3G0243000), we detected a 1,426-bp insertion in intron eight (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">7a</a>). The insertion showed high similarity to an unclassified transposable element (annotation noCAT_denovoHM-B-R7962-Map20; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM7">5c</a>). This repeat element is present 62 times in the HapOB genome, of which 20 insertions are full length and 4 are located in gene introns (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM9">7b</a>). Apart from this insertion and a few SNPs, no other differences were detected between the two alleles. In the OW progeny, all individuals that carry the transposable element insertion allele display the double flower phenotype (see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM13">11</a> for further details).</p><p>Phylogenetic analysis showed that RC3G0243000 belongs to the <i>APETALA2/TOE</i> clade within the <i>AP2/ERF</i> subfamily<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Shigyo, M., Hasebe, M. &amp; Ito, M. Molecular evolution of the AP2 subfamily. Gene 366, 256–265 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4097">39</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">7d</a>). Like all members of the AP2 clades, the protein encoded by RC3G0243000 contains two conserved AP2 domains and a conserved putative <i>miRNA172</i> binding site (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">7b,c</a>). The genomic position, expression analysis, protein sequence data and predicted deleterious effect of the insertion in intron 8 suggest that the <i>APETALA2/TOE</i> gene is a good candidate for the <i>DOUBLE FLOWER</i> locus. <i>APETALA2/TOE</i> has a central role in the establishment of the floral meristem and in the specification of floral organs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bowman, J. L., Alvarez, J., Weigel, D., Meyerowitz, E. M. &amp; Smyth, D. R. Control of flower development in Arabidopsis thaliana by APETALA1 and interacting genes. Development 119, 721–743 (1993)." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4120">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bowman, J. L., Smyth, D. R. &amp; Meyerowitz, E. M. Genes directing flower development in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 1, 37–52 (1989)." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4120_1">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jung, J.-H., Lee, S., Yun, J., Lee, M. &amp; Park, C.-M. The miR172 target TOE3 represses AGAMOUS expression during Arabidopsis floral patterning. Plant Sci. 215–216, 29–38 (2014)." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4120_2">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Zhang, B., Wang, L., Zeng, L., Zhang, C. &amp; Ma, H. Arabidopsis TOE proteins convey a photoperiodic signal to antagonize CONSTANS and regulate flowering time. Genes Dev. 29, 975–987 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4123">43</a></sup>. <i>APETALA2</i> was classified as a class A floral homeotic gene, which specifies sepal identity if expressed alone and petal identity if expressed together with class B genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Bowman, J. L., Smyth, D. R. &amp; Meyerowitz, E. M. Genetic interactions among floral homeotic genes of Arabidopsis. Development 112, 1–20 (1991)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4131">44</a></sup>. Furthermore, <i>AP2/TOE3</i> repressed <i>AGAMOUS</i> expression (a class C gene) in the two outer floral whorls in the floral meristem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Jung, J.-H., Lee, S., Yun, J., Lee, M. &amp; Park, C.-M. The miR172 target TOE3 represses AGAMOUS expression during Arabidopsis floral patterning. Plant Sci. 215–216, 29–38 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4141">42</a></sup> (reviewed in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Ó'Maoiléidigh, D. S., Graciet, E. &amp; Wellmer, F. Gene networks controlling Arabidopsis thaliana flower development. New Phytol. 201, 16–30 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4145">45</a></sup>). In rose, a reduction in the levels of <i>RhAGAMOUS</i> transcripts was proposed to be the basis of the double flower phenotype<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Dubois, A. et al. Tinkering with the C-function: a molecular frame for the selection of double flowers in cultivated roses. PLoS ONE 5, e9288 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4153">37</a></sup>. We hypothesise that misregulation of the rose <i>APETALA2/TOE</i> homologue (due to the presence of the transposable element) is responsible for the <i>RhAGAMOUS</i> transcript level reduction, leading to the double flower phenotype.</p><p>Interestingly, a GWAS approach for petal number (a quantitative analysis) in a panel of tetraploid and double flower varieties<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Schulz, D. F. et al. Genome-wide association analysis of the anthocyanin and carotenoid contents of rose petals. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 1798 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d39109e4167">33</a></sup> revealed that the most significant QTL is also located at the <i>DOUBLE FLOWER</i> locus (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#Fig4">4c</a>). Several markers in this cluster display significant dose-dependent effects on the number of petals. One of these markers, RhK5_4359_382 (at position 33.55 Mb), was analysed via the Kompetitive allele specific PCR (KASP) technology both in the original association panel of 96 cultivars and in an independent panel of 238 tetraploid varieties and showed the same effect in both populations (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">8a,b</a>). Two other markers (RhK5_14942 and RhMCRND_760_1045) were also tested on the 96 cultivars by KASP technology and revealed the same pattern (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0166-1#MOESM1">8c,d</a>). This demonstrates a dual role of the <i>DOUBLE FLOWER</i> locus in rose: it controls both the double flower phenotype (double versus single flowers) and the number of petals.
